     <p>The <b>Tesla Model S</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Full-size_car" title="Full-size car">full-sized</a> <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">all-electric</a> five-door, <a href="/wiki/Luxury_vehicle" title="Luxury vehicle">luxury</a> <a href="/wiki/Liftback" title="Liftback" class="mw-redirect">liftback</a>, produced by <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Motors" title="Tesla Motors">Tesla Motors</a>, and introduced in June 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-MSlaunch_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSlaunch-10">[10]</a></sup> It scored a perfect 5.0 <a href="/wiki/National_Highway_Traffic_Safety_Administration" title="National Highway Traffic Safety Administration">NHTSA</a> <a href="/wiki/Automobile_safety_rating" title="Automobile safety rating">automobile safety rating</a>. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a> (<a href="/wiki/FTP-75" title="FTP-75">EPA</a>) official range for the 2012 Model S Performance model equipped with an 85&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Watt-hour" title="Watt-hour" class="mw-redirect">kWh</a> (310&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Megajoule" title="Megajoule" class="mw-redirect">MJ</a>) <a href="/wiki/Battery_pack" title="Battery pack">battery pack</a> is 265 miles (426&nbsp;km), higher than any other electric car at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-EPAratings_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPAratings-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> EPA rates its energy consumption at 237.5&nbsp;watt·hour&nbsp;per&nbsp;kilometer (38&nbsp;kWh/100&nbsp;mi or 24&nbsp;kWh/100&nbsp;km) for a combined <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy</a> of 89 <a href="/wiki/Miles_per_gallon_gasoline_equivalent" title="Miles per gallon gasoline equivalent">miles per gallon gasoline equivalent</a> (2.64&nbsp;L/100&nbsp;km or 107&nbsp;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>).<sup id="cite_ref-EPAratings_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPAratings-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The Model S became the first electric car to top the monthly new car sales ranking in any country, twice leading in Norway, in September and again in December 2013;<sup id="cite_ref-SalesNorway092013_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SalesNorway092013-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NorwayTop1_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorwayTop1-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NorwayTop2_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorwayTop2-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NorwayTop3_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NorwayTop3-18">[18]</a></sup> and also in Denmark in December 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-Denmark2015_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denmark2015-19">[19]</a></sup> Global Model S sales passed the 100,000 unit milestone in December 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-ModelS100K_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModelS100K-20">[20]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The Model S ranked as the world's best selling <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicle" title="Plug-in electric vehicle">plug-in electric vehicle</a> in 2015, up from second best in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-Top2015Global_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Top2015Global-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Top102014_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Top102014-22">[22]</a></sup> The Model S also ranked as the top selling plug-in electric car in the U.S. in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-Sales2015US_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sales2015US-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ModelSTop2015US_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModelSTop2015US-24">[24]</a></sup> As of December 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tesla_Model_S&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the leading markets are the United States with a 60% share of global sales and Norway with 9.4%. Other leading country markets are China, the Netherlands, Canada, Denmark, Germany, and Switzerland .<sup id="cite_ref-Top2015Global_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Top2015Global-21">[21]</a></sup> As of December 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tesla_Model_S&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the Model S ranks as the world's second best selling plug-in car in history after the Nissan Leaf.<sup id="cite_ref-Top2015Global_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Top2015Global-21">[21]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The Tesla Model S won awards including the 2013 <a href="/wiki/World_Car_of_the_Year" title="World Car of the Year">World Green Car of the Year</a>, 2013 <a href="/wiki/Motor_Trend_Car_of_the_Year" title="Motor Trend Car of the Year">Motor Trend Car of the Year</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Automobile_(magazine)" title="Automobile (magazine)">Automobile</a></i> magazine's 2013 Car of the Year, <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time Magazine</a></i> Best 25 Inventions of the Year 2012 award and <i><a href="/wiki/Consumer_Reports" title="Consumer Reports">Consumer Reports</a>'</i> top-scoring car ever. In 2015, <i><a href="/wiki/Car_and_Driver" title="Car and Driver">Car and Driver</a></i> named the Model S the Car of the Century.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p> 
     <p></p>

     <p></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tesla_Model_S&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_Model_S_(108).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Tesla_Model_S_%28108%29.JPG/220px-Tesla_Model_S_%28108%29.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Tesla_Model_S_%28108%29.JPG/330px-Tesla_Model_S_%28108%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Tesla_Model_S_%28108%29.JPG/440px-Tesla_Model_S_%28108%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_Model_S_(108).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Tesla Model S prototype at the 2009 
        <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Motor_Show" title="Frankfurt Motor Show" class="mw-redirect">Frankfurt Motor Show</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The Model S was styled by <a href="/wiki/Franz_von_Holzhausen" title="Franz von Holzhausen">Franz von Holzhausen</a>, who previously worked for <a href="/wiki/Mazda_North_American_Operations" title="Mazda North American Operations">Mazda North American Operations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wert_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wert-26">[26]</a></sup> The car was codenamed <i>WhiteStar</i> during research and preliminary development.<sup id="cite_ref-Brian_Lam_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brian_Lam-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Merritt_Johnson_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merritt_Johnson-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Samuel_Abuelsamid_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Samuel_Abuelsamid-3">[3]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The Model S was announced in a press release on June 30, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-randt_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-randt-28">[28]</a></sup> The prototype vehicle was displayed at a press conference on March 26, 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-prototype_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prototype-29">[29]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In February 2008 it was reported that Tesla Motors was planning to offer a <a href="/wiki/Range_extender_(vehicle)" title="Range extender (vehicle)">range-extended</a> version of its Model S. This version would have included a <a href="/wiki/Gasoline_engine" title="Gasoline engine" class="mw-redirect">gasoline engine</a> to extend the driving range of the vehicle,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> but it was removed in later revisions. At the GoingGreen conference in September 2008 Tesla's CEO, <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">Elon Musk</a>, announced that Tesla was developing only electric cars.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:First_Tesla_Model_S_3rd_anniversary_2015.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/First_Tesla_Model_S_3rd_anniversary_2015.jpg/220px-First_Tesla_Model_S_3rd_anniversary_2015.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/First_Tesla_Model_S_3rd_anniversary_2015.jpg/330px-First_Tesla_Model_S_3rd_anniversary_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/First_Tesla_Model_S_3rd_anniversary_2015.jpg/440px-First_Tesla_Model_S_3rd_anniversary_2015.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5395" data-file-height="3488"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:First_Tesla_Model_S_3rd_anniversary_2015.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The first production Tesla Model S (with owner Tesla Board member 
        <a href="/wiki/Steve_Jurvetson" title="Steve Jurvetson">Steve Jurvetson</a>) in June 2015, three years after the car's market release.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Construction of an assembly factory in <a href="/wiki/Albuquerque,_New_Mexico" title="Albuquerque, New Mexico">Albuquerque, New Mexico</a> (a central location for shipping) was supposed to begin in April 2007, but was cancelled.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> A factory to be built in <a href="/wiki/San_Jose,_California" title="San Jose, California">San Jose, California</a> was also announced.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> In May 2010 Tesla announced it would produce the Model S at the former <a href="/wiki/NUMMI" title="NUMMI">NUMMI</a> assembly plant in Fremont, California,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> now known as the <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Factory" title="Tesla Factory">Tesla Factory</a>. This third plan was implemented.</p> 
     <p>In June 2015, three years after the Model S introduction and with almost 75,000 Model S sedans delivered worldwide, Tesla announced that Model S owners have accumulated over 1 billion electric miles (1.6 billion km) traveled, and saved more than half a million tons of CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-1BiMiles_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1BiMiles-38">[38]</a></sup> The Tesla Model S is the first plug-in electric vehicle to reach that milestone. In October 2014 General Motors reported that Volt owners had accumulated a total of 629 million all-electric miles (over 1 billion kilometers) out of a total of 1 billion miles (1.6 billion km) traveled; while Nissan reported in December 2014 that Leaf owners had traveled 625 million miles (1 billion kilometers).<sup id="cite_ref-1BiMiles_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1BiMiles-38">[38]</a></sup> Tesla also reported that 68% of Model S travel took place in North America, 25% in Europe and 7% in Asia-Pacific.<sup id="cite_ref-1BiMiles_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1BiMiles-38">[38]</a></sup> Global Model S sales passed the 100,000 unit milestone in December 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-ModelS100K_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModelS100K-20">[20]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2016, a racing series called the "Electric GT World Series" was promoted in preparation for a 2017 racing season using the P85+ as a race car on certain traditional tracks. The first season is planned to have 20 cars in 10 teams.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p> 
     <div style="clear:both;"></div> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tesla_Model_S&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Factory#Tesla_Model_S_manufacturing_process" title="Tesla Factory">Tesla Factory § Tesla Model S manufacturing process</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_auto_bots.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tesla_auto_bots.jpg/220px-Tesla_auto_bots.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tesla_auto_bots.jpg/330px-Tesla_auto_bots.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tesla_auto_bots.jpg/440px-Tesla_auto_bots.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5100" data-file-height="3564"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_auto_bots.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> A Tesla Model S being manufactured at the 
        <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Factory" title="Tesla Factory">Tesla Factory</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Tesla manufactures the Model S at the 5.4 million square foot<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Factory" title="Tesla Factory">Tesla Factory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fremont,_California" title="Fremont, California">Fremont, California</a>. For the European market, Tesla assembles and distributes from its European Distribution Center in <a href="/wiki/Tilburg" title="Tilburg">Tilburg</a>, the Netherlands.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Cars are built and tested in Fremont, California. The battery pack, the electric motor and parts are disassembled and shipped separately to Tilburg, where the cars are reassembled.<sup id="cite_ref-Tilburg_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tilburg-42">[42]</a></sup> The center occupies a 203,000&nbsp;sq&nbsp;ft (18,900&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) industrial building that also serves as a workshop and spare parts warehouse. Tesla expects the Model S to "pay back" the energy that went into producing the car in fewer than 10,000 miles (16,000&nbsp;km).<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The first ten customers received their cars at the Fremont factory on June 22, 2012 at the official launch.<sup id="cite_ref-InsideTesla_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InsideTesla-44">[44]</a></sup> Production grew from 15–20 cars completed/week in August 2012<sup id="cite_ref-InsideTesla_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InsideTesla-44">[44]</a></sup> to about 1,000 cars per week in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-te2015-05-02_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-te2015-05-02-45">[45]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In October 2015, Tesla Motors announced the company is negotiating with the Chinese government on producing its electric cars domestically. Local production has the potential to reduce the sales prices of Tesla models by a third.<sup id="cite_ref-China092015_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-China092015-46">[46]</a></sup> A Model S starts at about <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$76,000</span> in the U.S., while in China pricing starts at CN¥673,000, about <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$106,000</span>, after duties and other taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">Elon Musk</a> clarified that production will remain in the U.S., but if there's sufficient local demand for the <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Model_3" title="Tesla Model 3" class="mw-redirect">Tesla Model 3</a> in China, a factory could be built in the country as soon as a year after the launch of the new model. Production in Europe will also depend on the region's demand for the Model 3.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tesla_Model_S&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_Motors_Model_S_base.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Tesla_Motors_Model_S_base.JPG/220px-Tesla_Motors_Model_S_base.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Tesla_Motors_Model_S_base.JPG/330px-Tesla_Motors_Model_S_base.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Tesla_Motors_Model_S_base.JPG/440px-Tesla_Motors_Model_S_base.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2153" data-file-height="1431"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_Motors_Model_S_base.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Model S chassis, on display at 
        <a href="/wiki/Santana_Row" title="Santana Row">Santana Row</a> in 
        <a href="/wiki/San_Jose,_California" title="San Jose, California">San Jose, California</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The Model S exists in several versions, differing in energy capacity (battery size), power (motor size), and equipment.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Powertrain">Powertrain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tesla_Model_S&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Powertrain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_Model_S_motor_cutout.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Tesla_Model_S_motor_cutout.jpg/220px-Tesla_Model_S_motor_cutout.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Tesla_Model_S_motor_cutout.jpg/330px-Tesla_Model_S_motor_cutout.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Tesla_Model_S_motor_cutout.jpg/440px-Tesla_Model_S_motor_cutout.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_Model_S_motor_cutout.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Cutaway view of a Tesla Model S drive motor
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_Model_S_P85%2B_60_kW_Regenerative_Braking_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Tesla_Model_S_P85%2B_60_kW_Regenerative_Braking_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Tesla_Model_S_P85%2B_60_kW_Regenerative_Braking_%28cropped%29.jpg" width="220" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Tesla_Model_S_P85%2B_60_kW_Regenerative_Braking_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Tesla_Model_S_P85%2B_60_kW_Regenerative_Braking_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5c/Tesla_Model_S_P85%2B_60_kW_Regenerative_Braking_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="357" data-file-height="311"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_Model_S_P85%2B_60_kW_Regenerative_Braking_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> A Tesla Model S P85+ using 
        <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_braking" title="Regenerative braking" class="mw-redirect">regenerative braking</a> power in excess of 60 kW. During regenerative braking the power indicator is green
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The 2012 Tesla Model S Performance model has a <a href="/wiki/Three-phase_electric_power" title="Three-phase electric power">three phase</a>, four pole <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">AC</a> <a href="/wiki/Induction_motor" title="Induction motor">induction</a> 416&nbsp;hp (310&nbsp;kW) and 443&nbsp;ft·lb (600&nbsp;N·m) rear-mounted electric motor with copper <a href="/wiki/Rotor_(electric)" title="Rotor (electric)">rotor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-model_s_specs_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-model_s_specs-49">[49]</a></sup> The base model uses a 362&nbsp;hp (270&nbsp;kW) and 325&nbsp;ft·lb (440&nbsp;N·m) motor. The company claimed a <a href="/wiki/Drag_coefficient" title="Drag coefficient">drag coefficient</a> of <a href="/wiki/Automobile_drag_coefficient" title="Automobile drag coefficient"><i>C</i><small style="position:relative;top:4px;;right:1px;">d</small></a>=0.24,<sup id="cite_ref-DC_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DC-50">[50]</a></sup> lower than any car when released. Models of <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_CLA-Class" title="Mercedes-Benz CLA-Class">Mercedes-Benz CLA-Class</a> (released later) appeared to surpass the Model S<sup id="cite_ref-MT20120622_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MT20120622-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> however, independent measurement by <i><a href="/wiki/Car_And_Driver" title="Car And Driver" class="mw-redirect">Car And Driver</a></i> in May 2014 bore out Tesla's claim by exactly confirming a drag coefficient of <i>C</i><small style="position:relative;top:4px;;right:1px;">d</small>=0.24, but in the same test, measured the Mercedes CLA at <i>C</i><small style="position:relative;top:4px;;right:1px;">d</small>=0.30, putting Mercedes' claim into question.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The rear axle has a traditional <a href="/wiki/Open_differential" title="Open differential" class="mw-redirect">open differential</a>. Models with dual motors also have an open differential on the front axles as well. The front and rear axles have no mechanical linkage – with dual motors the power distribution among them is controlled <a href="/wiki/Electronic_differential" title="Electronic differential">electronically</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The discontinued Model S P85 top speed is 130&nbsp;mph (210&nbsp;km/h) and it accelerates from 0 to 60 miles per hour (0 to 97&nbsp;km/h) in <span class="nowrap">4.2 seconds</span>. The replacement Model S 85D has a top speed of 155&nbsp;mph (249&nbsp;km/h) and it accelerates from 0 to 60 miles per hour (0 to 97&nbsp;km/h) in <span class="nowrap">4.2 seconds</span>, despite the lower total motor power, in part due to the improved traction of the all-wheel drive powertrain. The Model S P85D, a dual motor all-wheel drive vehicle has a <a href="/wiki/Speed_limiter#Fixed" title="Speed limiter">governed top speed</a> of 155&nbsp;mph (249&nbsp;km/h)<sup id="cite_ref-cdsP85Dlong_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cdsP85Dlong-54">[54]</a></sup> and it accelerates from 0 to 60 miles per hour (0 to 97&nbsp;km/h) in <span class="nowrap">3.2 seconds</span> (tested to <span class="nowrap">3.1 seconds</span>), under "Insane Mode", with 1<a href="/wiki/Gee_force" title="Gee force" class="mw-redirect">g</a> of acceleration.<sup id="cite_ref-cdsP85Dlong_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cdsP85Dlong-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> New P85Ds have an optional "Ludicrous Mode" hardware package available with the 90 kWh battery upgrade (thus becoming a P90D) that improves the 0 to 60 miles per hour (0 to 97&nbsp;km/h) acceleration to <span class="nowrap">2.8 seconds</span> and 1.1g.<sup id="cite_ref-TMspecsBat_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TMspecsBat-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The powertrain also provides regenerative braking power of more than 60&nbsp;kW which reduces both energy consumption and improves brake lifetime.</p> 
     <p>In July 2015, Tesla announced its goal to make the Model S powertrain last for one million miles.<sup id="cite_ref-autoblog20150715_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autoblog20150715-59">[59]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dual-motor_all-wheel-drive_versions">Dual-motor all-wheel-drive versions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tesla_Model_S&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Dual-motor all-wheel-drive versions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>On October 9, 2014, Tesla announced the introduction of All Wheel Drive (AWD) versions of the Model S's 60, 85, and P85 models, designated by a <i>D</i> at the end of the model number.<sup id="cite_ref-BO_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BO-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IND_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IND-62">[62]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>On April 8, 2015, Tesla introduced the Model S 70D as its new <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$75,000</span> entry-level car, with all-wheel drive and an improved range of 240 miles (385&nbsp;km). The 70D replaced the 60 and 60D in the Model S lineup, with the latter two models no longer being available for purchase. The P85 option was also dropped.<sup id="cite_ref-David_Noland_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_Noland-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In the 85D, the rear drive unit is replaced by a smaller one to save cost and weight, while a second motor of similar size is added to the front wheels. This results in an <a href="/wiki/Four-wheel_drive" title="Four-wheel drive">AWD</a> car with comparable power and acceleration to the RWD version while keeping the price increase to <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$5,000</span>. Additionally, the 85D reports a 2% (5-mile) range increase and 11% increase in top speed over the 85<sup id="cite_ref-my.teslamotors.com_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-my.teslamotors.com-65">[65]</a></sup> (presumably due to incremental improvements in hardware and software). In the P85D, the high-power rear drive unit is retained, while a new front drive motor boosts the total power by about 50%. This results in a significant increase in acceleration and top speed. The 85&nbsp;kWh version rose from <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$81,070</span> to <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$86,070</span>, while the P85 (now the P85D) jumped from <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$94,570</span> to <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$105,670</span>. Deliveries of the P85D started in December 2014, with the 85D models starting in February 2015, and the 70D models starting in April 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-61">[61]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battery">Battery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tesla_Model_S&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Battery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <table class="infobox" style="width:22em;float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em"> 
      <caption>
       External images
      </caption> 
      <tbody>
       <tr> 
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/16px-Searchtool.svg.png" width="16" height="16" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/24px-Searchtool.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/32px-Searchtool.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512"> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.teslamotors.com/sites/default/files/blog_images/driving-range-for-the-model-s-family-chart1.png">Speed-dependent ranges of various Model S</a></td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/16px-Searchtool.svg.png" width="16" height="16" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/24px-Searchtool.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/61/Searchtool.svg/32px-Searchtool.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512"> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.teslamotors.com/sites/default/files/graph1.jpg">Speed-dependent ranges, Roadster &amp; Model S</a></td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Lithium-Ion_Cell_cylindric.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Lithium-Ion_Cell_cylindric.JPG/220px-Lithium-Ion_Cell_cylindric.JPG" width="220" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Lithium-Ion_Cell_cylindric.JPG/330px-Lithium-Ion_Cell_cylindric.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Lithium-Ion_Cell_cylindric.JPG/440px-Lithium-Ion_Cell_cylindric.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2737" data-file-height="2532"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Lithium-Ion_Cell_cylindric.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Cylindrical cell (18650) prior to assembly. Several thousand of them form the Tesla Model S battery (see 
        <a href="/wiki/Gigafactory_1#Background" title="Gigafactory 1">Gigafactory</a>).
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The 60&nbsp;kWh <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle_battery" title="Electric vehicle battery">electric vehicle battery</a> was rated to deliver 230 miles (370&nbsp;km), while an 85&nbsp;kWh battery was rated at 320 miles (510&nbsp;km), assuming a constant speed of 55&nbsp;mph (89&nbsp;km/h).<sup id="cite_ref-TMspecsBat_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TMspecsBat-56">[56]</a></sup> In 2012, the EPA <a href="/wiki/All-electric_range" title="All-electric range">range</a> for the 60&nbsp;kWh battery pack model is 208&nbsp;mi (335&nbsp;km)<sup id="cite_ref-Range60kWh_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Range60kWh-66">[66]</a></sup> and the 85&nbsp;kWh battery is 265 miles (426&nbsp;km).<sup id="cite_ref-EPAratings_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPAratings-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> According to Musk, the Model S has a battery with twice the energy density of that on the Nissan Leaf, but the difference in range is more than double. This is also due to other factors such as drag coefficient, weight, engine efficiency and rolling resistance.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Musk stated that driving at 65&nbsp;mph (105&nbsp;km/h), under normal conditions, gives a reasonable range of 250 miles (400&nbsp;km).<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_Model_S_with_hood_up.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Tesla_Model_S_with_hood_up.JPG/220px-Tesla_Model_S_with_hood_up.JPG" width="220" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Tesla_Model_S_with_hood_up.JPG/330px-Tesla_Model_S_with_hood_up.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Tesla_Model_S_with_hood_up.JPG/440px-Tesla_Model_S_with_hood_up.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2376"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_Model_S_with_hood_up.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Tesla Model S showing the front trunk where the engine would be in a typical car. Tesla Motors calls this a 
        <i>frunk</i>.
        <sup id="cite_ref-TMspecs_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TMspecs-70">[70]</a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The energy-saving sleep state powers off the display and other vehicle electronics, after the car goes to sleep. This increases the time it takes the touchscreen and instrument panel to become usable. This mode can decrease the loss of the car's range when not being used (2.3&nbsp;mi, 3.7&nbsp;km per day, as of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tesla_Model_S&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-vampire_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vampire-71">[71]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The 85&nbsp;kWh battery pack weighs 1,200&nbsp;lb (540&nbsp;kg)<sup id="cite_ref-rope_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rope-72">[72]</a></sup> and contains 7,104 <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">lithium-ion battery</a> <a href="/wiki/18650" title="18650" class="mw-redirect">cells</a> in 16 modules<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> wired in series (14 in the flat section and two stacked on the front).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Each module contains 6 groups<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> of 74 cells<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-76">[76]</a></sup> wired in parallel; the 6 groups are then wired in series within the module.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT062312_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT062312-79">[79]</a></sup> As of June 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tesla_Model_S&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the battery pack used modified <a href="/wiki/Panasonic" title="Panasonic">Panasonic</a> cells with nickel-cobalt-aluminum <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">cathodes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MT20120622_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MT20120622-4">[4]</a></sup> Each cell was of the 18650 form factor (i.e., an 18&nbsp;mm diameter, 65&nbsp;mm height cylinder), similar to the Panasonic NCR18650B cell that has an energy density of 265&nbsp;Wh/kg.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Analysts estimate battery cost to be around 21–22% of the car cost.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> List price for a replacement battery was <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$44,000</span> in February 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-noland-loss1_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-noland-loss1-82">[82]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The battery is guaranteed for eight years or 125,000 miles (200,000&nbsp;km in metric countries) for the base model with the 60&nbsp;kWh battery pack. The 85&nbsp;kWh battery pack is guaranteed for eight years and unlimited miles.<sup id="cite_ref-TMOptions_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TMOptions-83">[83]</a></sup> A poll among drivers indicate that accumulated battery loss steadies around 5% after 30,000 miles (50,000&nbsp;km),<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> decreasing further about 1% per additional 30,000 miles. Unlike Nissan, Tesla does not specify a limit for battery loss, but some early battery packs have been replaced.<sup id="cite_ref-noland-loss1_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-noland-loss1-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>A separate battery replacement guarantee takes effect after the eighth year at a cost of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$10,000</span> for the 60&nbsp;kWh battery and <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$12,000</span> for the 85&nbsp;kWh battery.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2013, Tesla canceled a 40&nbsp;kWh version of the car due to lack of demand, stating that only 4% of pre-orders were for the 40&nbsp;kWh battery option. Customers who ordered this option instead received the 60&nbsp;kWh pack, with charge software-limited to 40&nbsp;kWh (139 miles, 224&nbsp;km<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>). It has the improved acceleration and top speed of the bigger pack and can be upgraded to use the full 60&nbsp;kWh for <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$11,000</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>On April 8, 2015, Tesla Motors discontinued the Model S 60, and replaced the base model with the Model S 70.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In July 2015, Tesla introduced its 70, 90, 90D and P90D variants along with a "ludicrous mode" for the performance model. The P90D combines a front axle power of 259 horsepower (193&nbsp;kW) and rear axle power of 503 horsepower (375&nbsp;kW) to a total of 762 horsepower (568&nbsp;kW), for a 0–60&nbsp;mph time of 2.8 sec. The acceleration of the P90D can reach 1.1<a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force">g</a>, described by Tesla as "faster than falling".<sup id="cite_ref-autoblog20150715_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autoblog20150715-59">[59]</a></sup> The "ludicrous mode" costs <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$10,000</span> for P90D, but is discounted to <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$5,000</span> plus labor for P85D owners until January 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-musk2015-07-17_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-musk2015-07-17-90">[90]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2015, Tesla introduced a 70&nbsp;kWh battery to replace the existing 60&nbsp;kWh batteries and base 60&nbsp;kWh Model S vehicles, as the 60 was low margin and not sufficiently welcomed by customers.<sup id="cite_ref-2015ecP8_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015ecP8-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2015ecP9_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015ecP9-92">[92]</a></sup> All 70&nbsp;kWh cars can be had with rear-wheel drive or all wheel drive.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In July 2015, Tesla introduced its 90 and P90D, a "range upgrade" of the 85 variants with a 90&nbsp;kWh battery and explained the 6% energy increase with "improved cell chemistry"<sup id="cite_ref-autoblog20150715_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autoblog20150715-59">[59]</a></sup> and with the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> into the graphite cell <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anode</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Tesla additionally announced its expectation of an annual improvement in battery capacity of about 5%. Tesla also explained that apart from the P90D the Model S has its main battery conductor protected by a <a href="/wiki/Fuse_(electrical)" title="Fuse (electrical)">fuse</a> rated for 1,300&nbsp;A and that this rating is somewhat conservative given the uncertainty of the exact conditions under which a fuse melts. To reduce this uncertainty Tesla has for the P90D introduced <a href="/wiki/Inconel" title="Inconel">Inconel</a> battery contactors<sup id="cite_ref-ludiInco_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ludiInco-95">[95]</a></sup> and an electronically controlled pyro-activated fuse, which monitors the current at the millisecond level, cuts the power with "extreme precision and certainty" and has a rating of 1,500&nbsp;A.<sup id="cite_ref-autoblog20150715_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autoblog20150715-59">[59]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In February 2016, the 85 kWh battery option was discontinued in countries including the USA, Australia and Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In March 2016, media reported that a firmware version distributed for the Tesla Model S has support for a yet to come P100D variant, i.e. a performance model with a 100 kWh battery and dual motors.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Specifications">Specifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tesla_Model_S&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Specifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_consumption">Energy consumption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tesla_Model_S&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Energy consumption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Under its five-cycle testing protocol, the US <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="Environmental Protection Agency" class="mw-redirect">Environmental Protection Agency</a> (EPA) rated the 85 kWh Model S model with a combined <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy</a> equivalent of 89&nbsp;MPGe (2.64&nbsp;L/100&nbsp;km), with an equivalent 88&nbsp;mpg<sub><small>-US</small></sub> (2.7&nbsp;L/100&nbsp;km; 106&nbsp;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>) in city driving and 90&nbsp;mpg<sub><small>-US</small></sub> (2.6&nbsp;L/100&nbsp;km; 110&nbsp;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>) on highways.<sup id="cite_ref-EPAratings_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPAratings-11">[11]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The following table shows the EPA's official ratings for fuel economy in <a href="/wiki/Miles_per_gallon_gasoline_equivalent" title="Miles per gallon gasoline equivalent">miles per gallon gasoline equivalent</a> (MPGe) and EPA's estimated out-of-pocket fuel costs for the variants of the Model S rated as of 30 December 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tesla_Model_S&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> and as displayed in the <a href="/wiki/Monroney_label" title="Monroney label" class="mw-redirect">Monroney label</a>.</p>
